Per-Domain Analysis Table 5 presents per-domain PQS across all ten domains. With Kimi K2.5, PlanTwin achieves PQS ranging from 0.67 (frontend) to 1.00 (data pipeline). Data pipeline tasks yield perfect PQS because their structured ETL workflows align naturally with the typed abstract graph representation. Database (0.90) and API integration (0.91) tasks also achieve high PQS, confirming that the projection pipeline generalizes well to structured integration workflows. Cross-planner comparison reveals that Kimi, MiniMax, and GLM produce similar per-domain profiles: data pipeline (1.00/1.00/0.98), API integration (0.91/0.81/0.83), and database (0.90/0.72/0.93). The largest inter-planner variation occurs on security IR, where Kimi (0.74) and GLM (0.80) outperform MiniMax (0.70) and Gemini (0.48), suggesting that multi-step reasoning over the structured twin benefits from stronger planners. On DevOps tasks, all four planners are tightly clustered (0.72/0.72/0.83/0.72), while data pipeline tasks yield near-perfect PQS across the board, confirming that highly structured ETL workflows are planner-agnostic. PII redaction's SND varies sharply by domain: coding tasks achieve only SND = 0.12 (regex misses 88% of code-embedded secrets), while documents and API integration reach SND = 1.0. Database tasks show intermediate SND = 0.73, where regex partially captures connection strings but misses embedded PGP keys. PlanTwin maintains SND = 1.0 across all ten domains without exception, as the architectural guarantee is independent of sensitive-item distribution. Domain generalization. PlanTwin generalizes across ten domains and four cloud planners, sustaining snd = 1.0 =1.0 with PQS of 0.67-1.00. Consistent domain-level trends indicate planner-agnostic privacy-preserving projection. 5.6. Adversarial Re-identification. We empirically evaluate the re-identification risk of the twin's schema fields through a game-based experiment aligned with Goal 1 (§ 3.2). An adversary with auxiliary knowledge observes a target object's twin and attempts to match it to the correct file in a candidate pool of size k k . We model four adversary strategies with increasing auxiliary knowledge: (1) Random guessing (baseline), (2) Kind-only matching using the file type field, (3) Kind+Semantic matching using both kind and semantic_class, and (4) Full fingerprint matching using all disclosed twin fields ( kind, semantic_class, contains, sensitivity). Each strategy selects the candidate that maximizes field overlap with the target twin. We run 500 trials with pool size k = 15 k=15 and 5 target objects per trial, drawing from a diverse template library spanning coding, configuration, documentation, and infrastructure files. Fig. 4 presents the results. Random guessing achieves 6.6% accuracy, matching the theoretical 1 / k = 6.7 % 1/k=6.7% . Knowing only the file type ( kind) increases accuracy to 33.5%, because the six kind categories already partition the pool into smaller anonymity sets. Adding the semantic_class field raises accuracy to 78.6%, since semantic labels such as authentication_module or payment_service are highly identifying. With the full fingerprint, accuracy reaches 94.1% (MRR = 0.973), confirming that the twin's combined schema fields carry substantial re-identification potential when disclosed without budget control. To evaluate how the anonymity set size affects re-identification risk, we repeat the experiment with pool sizes k ∈ { 5 , 10 , 15 , 20 , 25 , 30 } k\in{5,10,15,20,25,30} (300 trials each). Fig. 6 shows the results. Random accuracy scales as 1 / k 1/k , decreasing from 19.3% ( k = 5 k=5 ) to 4.3% ( k = 30 k=30 ). Kind-only and Kind+Semantic strategies degrade with increasing k k , dropping from 63.1% and 94.1% at k = 5 k=5 to 19.7% and 63.3% at k = 30 k=30 , respectively. However, the full fingerprint adversary remains above 88.3% even at k = 30 k=30 , indicating that the twin's combined fields are inherently identifying regardless of pool size. This result empirically motivates the disclosure budget mechanism (§ 4.4): absent per-object budget limits, an adversary with auxiliary knowledge can re-identify files from the twin alone with high confidence. Re-identification risk. Without disclosure budget control, an adversary with full auxiliary knowledge achieves 94.1% re-identification accuracy ( k = 15 k=15 ) and > > 88% even at k = 30 k=30 . This empirically validates the need for per-object disclosure budgets: individual schema fields are not identifying, but their combination creates a unique fingerprint. 5.7. Pipeline Stage Ablation We isolate the contribution of each pipeline stage by running four variants: (1) Full (all four stages), (2) No Redaction (skip Stage 2, sensitive entity redaction), (3) No Generalize (skip Stage 3, value generalization), and (4) Extract+Schema (Stages 1–2 only, extract metadata and build typed graph but skip generalization and schema projection). All variants use Kimi K2.5 with plan-aware prompting on the same 58-task benchmark. Fig. 5 presents the results. Across all four variants, SND remains 1.0, confirming that the typed abstract graph representation provides architectural data isolation regardless of whether downstream redaction or generalization stages are active. PQS ranges from 0.773 (Full) to 0.790 (No Redaction), a spread of only 0.017. Skipping redaction yields a marginal PQS improvement (+2.2%) because the planner receives slightly richer metadata fields, while skipping generalization has negligible effect (+0.2%). The Extract+Schema variant (0.788) performs comparably to Full, suggesting that the core utility derives from the structured twin representation itself rather than the privacy-hardening stages. Figure 5. Pipeline stage ablation across four variants. All maintain SND = 1.0, while PQS stays within 0.773–0.790, indicating minimal utility loss from privacy hardening. Error bars show per-task standard deviation. Per-domain analysis reveals that data pipeline tasks achieve PQS = 1.00 across all four variants, while coding tasks show the largest inter-variant spread (0.534–0.753), reflecting their higher sensitivity to metadata granularity. Normalized leakage (NL) is lowest for No Redaction (0.553) and Extract+Schema (0.566), as fewer pipeline stages consume less disclosure budget per object. Pipeline ablation. The privacy-hardening stages (redaction, generalization) impose < < 2.2% PQS cost while the typed abstract graph representation alone guarantees SND = 1.0. The full 4-stage pipeline offers the best privacy–utility balance: minimal utility loss with maximum defense-in-depth. 5.8. Budget Threshold Sensitivity We vary the disclosure budget threshold by applying scale factors α ∈ { 0.25 , 0.5 , 0.75 , 1.0 , 1.5 } \alpha\in{0.25,0.5,0.75,1.0,1.5} to the default per-object budget τ  ( o ) \tau(o) , yielding effective thresholds α ⋅ τ  ( o ) \alpha\cdot\tau(o) . Lower α \alpha restricts disclosure more aggressively; higher α \alpha permits more fields to be revealed. Fig. 7 shows the privacy–utility trade-off. At α = 0.25 \alpha=0.25 (quarter budget), PQS drops to 0.513 as the gatekeeper aggressively withholds twin fields, starving the planner of context. Doubling the budget to α = 0.5 \alpha=0.5 recovers PQS to 0.639. At α = 0.75 \alpha=0.75 , PQS reaches 0.801, matching the default ( α = 1.0 \alpha=1.0 , PQS = 0.801) and indicating that the default threshold already provides near-saturated utility. Increasing to α = 1.5 \alpha=1.5 yields PQS = 0.783, a slight decrease likely due to stochastic variation rather than a meaningful trend; SND remains 1.0 throughout. Normalized leakage exhibits an inverted-U pattern: NL peaks at α = 0.75 \alpha=0.75 (0.777) then decreases at α = 1.0 \alpha=1.0 (0.626) and α = 1.5 \alpha=1.5 (0.466). This occurs because larger budgets leave more unused headroom per object, reducing the fraction of budget consumed. The practical implication is that α ∈ [ 0.75 , 1.0 ] \alpha\in[0.75,1.0] represents a sweet spot: PQS is saturated while NL remains moderate. Figure 6. Re-identification accuracy versus anonymity set size k k . Full fingerprint matching remains above 88% even at k = 30 k=30 , while weaker strategies degrade with larger pools. Shaded regions denote ± 1 \pm 1 std. dev. Figure 7. Budget threshold sensitivity versus scale factor α \alpha . PQS saturates at α ≥ 0.75 \alpha\geq 0.75 , while normalized leakage peaks at α = 0.75 \alpha=0.75 and then declines as larger budgets leave more headroom. Shading denotes ± 1 \pm 1 std. dev. in PQS. Budget sensitivity. PQS saturates at α ≥ 0.75 \alpha\geq 0.75 , confirming that the default budget threshold is not over-restrictive. Reducing the budget to α = 0.25 \alpha=0.25 halves PQS, while α = 1.5 \alpha=1.5 provides no additional utility. The sweet spot α ∈ [ 0.75 , 1.0 ] \alpha\in[0.75,1.0] achieves PQS > > 0.80 with NL < < 0.78. 5.9. Multi-Turn Budget Depletion We evaluate the disclosure budget mechanism under multi-turn usage, where the middleware persists across all 58 tasks and budget consumption accumulates. This simulates a realistic deployment where an agent processes a sequence of tasks over a session, progressively depleting per-object budgets. Fig. 8 compares per-domain PQS. Overall PQS decreases from 0.773 (single-turn) to 0.767 (multi-turn), a modest − - 0.8% degradation. The largest drops occur on coding ( − - 0.233, from 0.753 to 0.520) and data pipeline ( − - 0.167, from 1.000 to 0.833), where objects are accessed across multiple tasks, depleting their budgets and forcing the gatekeeper to withhold fields. Conversely, document (+0.133), security IR (+0.185), and database (+0.006) tasks show slight PQS increases in multi-turn mode, because the planner benefits from residual context in the persistent twin graph. Fig. 9 reveals the privacy side: multi-turn drives NL to 1.0 across all ten domains, compared to a mean of 0.611 in single-turn mode. This confirms that cumulative disclosure fully exhausts per-object budgets over a 58-task session. Importantly, SND remains 1.0 in both modes—the budget mechanism caps total leakage at τ  ( o ) \tau(o) per object without ever exposing raw content. During the multi-turn run, 8 objects were excluded entirely (“budget exceeded at registration”), demonstrating the gatekeeper's enforcement of hard budget limits. Multi-turn depletion Multi-turn budget accumulation lowers PQS by 0.8%, while NL reaches 1.0. The system degrades gracefully: over-budget objects are excluded rather than leaking additional information, and SND stays at 1.0 throughout.
Discussion We now discuss why PlanTwin provides privacy benefits beyond execution isolation alone, what leakage channels remain, how the design scales with environment and workflow size, and the main limitations of the current study. 6.1. Privacy and Security Discussion PlanTwin does not aim to make cloud planning cryptographically private. Instead, it reduces exposure architecturally by constraining what the planner can observe through schema projection, bounded capabilities, and cumulative disclosure control. Content reconstruction. For raw-content reconstruction, the design is structurally restrictive. The cloud never receives raw files, free-form text, paths, credentials, or unsanitized execution outputs. All planner-visible state is produced by the projection pipeline and expressed through bounded schema fields. As a result, the cloud-visible channel excludes raw content by construction rather than attempting to scrub it after the fact. Reconstruction therefore becomes possible only if the local projection itself emits overly specific attributes. Re-identification. For object re-identification (Goal 1), privacy depends not only on the schema vocabulary but also on how much of it is disclosed over time. As shown in § 5.6, disclosing all twin fields allows an adversary to recover object identity with high confidence. This means the twin is not intrinsically anonymous. Instead, privacy comes from partial observability: the gatekeeper prevents the planner from accumulating a complete object fingerprint by enforcing per-object disclosure budgets. In this sense, the budget is a core security mechanism rather than a secondary optimization. Cross-session linkage. For cross-session linkage (Goal 2), fresh random object identifiers prevent trivial reuse of IDs across sessions. However, identifier randomization alone is insufficient. Repeated disclosure of rare semantic or relational patterns may still support linkage if the same object induces a distinctive signature across sessions. In practice, unlinkability is improved by the combination of session-local IDs, bounded vocabularies, and cumulative disclosure limits. The goal is not to make objects indistinguishable in principle, but to prevent the planner from observing enough stable attributes to link them reliably. Structural inference. For structural inference (Goal 3), PlanTwin reduces but does not eliminate leakage. Graph topology is itself informative: unusual dependency patterns, rare capability requirements, or asymmetric connectivity may reveal properties of the underlying project even when node attributes are sanitized. Bounded edge vocabularies and disclosure budgets reduce this signal, but cannot remove it entirely without also reducing planning utility. This is a structural trade-off rather than an implementation artifact. Prompt- and plan-level attack surfaces. The design also narrows two common attack surfaces. First, prompt-injection opportunities are reduced because the cloud planner never receives raw local artifacts, only schema-constrained abstractions. Second, unsafe or privacy-invasive plans are contained locally: every step is mediated by capability validation, policy checks, disclosure accounting, and optional human approval. Even if the planner proposes an overreaching plan, enforcement remains on the trusted local side. Figure 8. Per-domain PQS under single- and multi-turn budgeting. Multi-turn most degrades data-pipeline and coding tasks. Repeated access to the same objects exhausts budgets. Figure 9. Per-domain normalized leakage in single-/multi-turn modes. Multi-turn drives NL to 1.0 across domains. 6.2. Complexity and Scalability Let n n be the number of tracked objects, m m the number of typed edges in the twin graph, and k k the number of plan steps. Let | F i | |F_{i}| denote the token volume of object i i , and let d d be the average number of retained schema fields per object. Projection cost. Stage 1 dominates local preprocessing when SLM-backed extraction is used, with cost O  ( ∑ i | F i | ⋅ c SLM ) , O!\left(\sum_{i}|F_{i}|\cdot c_{\text{SLM}}\right), where c SLM c_{\text{SLM}} is the effective per-token extraction cost. Stages 2–4 are linear in the number of retained fields and relations, O  ( n  d + m ) , O(nd+m), since redaction, generalization, and schema projection operate over object-local metadata and typed graph structure. Budget and gatekeeping cost. Disclosure accounting scales with the number of newly referenced objects and fields per turn. Under bounded capability arity, this is effectively O  ( 1 ) O(1) per step and O  ( k ) O(k) per planning cycle; under a more general formulation it is upper bounded by O  ( n ) O(n) if every tracked object must be checked. Local validation and execution contribute O  ( k  α ) O(k\alpha) , where α \alpha is the average cost of one validated capability execution. Cloud planning cost. Cloud planning is dominated by model inference over the sanitized twin and prompt state, which we write abstractly as O  ( L ) O(L) , where L L captures planner inference cost. Because the twin is much smaller and more structured than raw context, this cost depends primarily on the size of the abstract state and the complexity of the requested workflow rather than the full volume of local artifacts. Combining these terms, the per-cycle cost is O  ( ∑ i | F i | ⋅ c SLM + n  d + m + L + k  α ) . O!\left(\sum_{i}|F_{i}|\cdot c_{\text{SLM}}+nd+m+L+k\alpha\right). In practice, this yields two main regimes. For large local environments, Stage 1 extraction dominates because the local side must summarize many raw artifacts into schema form. For smaller environments or long-horizon workflows, cloud inference dominates. This matches the empirical results in § 5.4, where total latency is driven primarily by SLM extraction and cloud API time, while deterministic projection and gatekeeping add negligible overhead. Scalability implication. The deterministic parts of the pipeline scale well, but end-to-end latency is bounded by whichever reasoning layer is active: local extraction for environment-heavy workloads, or cloud planning for reasoning-heavy workloads. Capability design and projection selectivity therefore matter not only for privacy, but also for performance. 6.2.1. Toward Production Deployment. The current prototype transmits the sanitized twin Z t Z_{t} as a single JSON payload to the cloud planner, which reasons declaratively over the abstract graph without any execution environment. A natural next step is cloud-side sandbox construction: upon receiving Z t Z_{t} , the cloud instantiates an ephemeral container whose directory structure, file stubs, and type annotations mirror the schema graph. Concretely, each node in the typed abstract graph G t G_{t} maps to a synthetic artifact (e.g., a code file containing only the declared semantic_class, import stubs matching the contains tags, and size-appropriate placeholder bodies). The planner then iterates with this sandbox twin, running linters, test harnesses, and static analyzers before producing a verified plan. This design raises a synthetic-population privacy trade-off: the sandbox must contain enough semantic structure for meaningful tool execution, yet each synthetic artifact is derived solely from the bounded schema fields already disclosed under the budget τ  ( o ) \tau(o) . We give a concise privacy argument showing that the sandbox construction preserves all existing guarantees. Proposition 6.1 (Synthetic-population privacy bound). Let Synth : 𝒵 → 𝒮 \operatorname{Synth}:\mathcal{Z}\to\mathcal{S} be a (possibly randomized) sandbox generator that takes as input only the sanitized twin Z t = Π  ( X t ) Z_{t}=\Pi(X_{t}) . Let R 1 : T R_{1:T} denote the sequence of tool outputs produced by the planner interacting with the sandbox content S = Synth  ( Z t ) S=\operatorname{Synth}(Z_{t}) , where the planner's interaction policy is a function of S S alone and does not incorporate auxiliary knowledge correlated with X t X_{t} beyond what Z t Z_{t} already discloses. Then: (12) X t → Z t → S → R 1 : T . X_{t}\to Z_{t}\to S\to R_{1:T}. By the Markov property (conditional independence): (13) I  ( X t ; S , R 1 : T ∣ Z t ) = 0 , I(X_{t};,S,R_{1:T}\mid Z_{t})=0, i.e., the sandbox and all interaction transcripts are conditionally independent of the real environment given Z t Z_{t} . Consequently: (1) The ( k , δ ) (k,\delta) -anonymity bound (Theorem 1) holds with identical parameters, since the adversary's posterior over object identity is fully determined by Z t Z_{t} . (2) The ϵ \epsilon -unlinkability bound (Theorem 2) holds by the post-processing property of D ∞ D_{\infty} : for any (randomized) function f f , D ∞  ( f  ( M  ( o ) ) ∥ f  ( M  ( o ′ ) ) ) ≤ D ∞  ( M  ( o ) ∥ M  ( o ′ ) ) D_{\infty}(f(M(o))|f(M(o^{\prime})))\leq D_{\infty}(M(o)|M(o^{\prime})) (see Appendix B, Theorem 2). (3) The composition bound (Theorem 3) is unchanged, since sandbox interactions consume no additional budget beyond the fields already disclosed in Z t Z_{t} . Two assumptions matter here. First, Synth \operatorname{Synth} uses only Z t Z_{t} : it has no access to the real environment, no external knowledge base conditioned on the user's identity, and no retrieval-augmented generation over corpora that could correlate with the original content. Second, the planner's interaction policy within the sandbox must not incorporate side information correlated with X t X_{t} beyond what Z t Z_{t} discloses (e.g., through pretraining memorization of specific codebases). Violations of either assumption break the Markov property and require separate analysis. From an engineering perspective, lightweight sandbox runtimes such as E2B (E2B, 2025) (cold start ∼ {\sim} 150 ms, per-request ephemeral lifecycle) or gVisor-based isolation (SkyPilot, 2025) provide the necessary infrastructure. The sandbox is destroyed after each planning cycle, ensuring no persistent state accumulates across sessions. Integrating sandbox construction with the existing MCP capability interface (Anthropic, 2024) follows naturally from the architecture: each declared capability maps to a sandbox-side tool endpoint, and the gatekeeper validates sandbox tool invocations against the same policy as direct execution. Stateful tools and asynchronous invocations require additional scoping logic that we do not address here. We leave empirical evaluation of sandbox-assisted planning quality and the design of Synth \operatorname{Synth} generators that maximize tool utility within the privacy bound of Proposition 6.1 to future work.
Conclusion We presented PlanTwin, a system for privacy-preserving cloud-assisted LLM planning. Instead of exposing raw local context to a strong remote planner, PlanTwin restricts planning to a bounded abstract view generated locally and enforced through capability mediation and multi-turn disclosure budgets. PlanTwin achieves full sensitive-item non-disclosure while maintaining planning quality close to full-context cloud planning across 60 tasks, outperforming both simple redaction and local weak-model baselines in privacy-utility trade-offs. Our design is planner-agnostic and remains robust under ablations and budget-constrained multi-turn interaction. Our results show that strong cloud planning does NOT require raw-context exposure. Planning-time observability should therefore be treated as a first-class systems interface in privacy-aware agents. Table 7. Notation. Upper part: system components. Lower part: privacy analysis. Appendix A Disclosure Cost Accounting The weighted field-cost model assigns each schema field a base cost w f w_{f} reflecting its re-identification potential. For an object o o with attribute set A  ( o ) A(o) , the initial disclosure cost is (14) Δ 0  ( o ) = ∑ f ∈ A  ( o ) w f . \Delta_{0}(o)=\sum_{f\in A(o)}w_{f}. For subsequent turns, only new reveals incur additional cost: (15) Δ t  ( o ) = ∑ f ∈ A t  ( o ) ∖ A 1 : t − 1  ( o ) w f , \Delta_{t}(o)=\sum_{f\in A_{t}(o)\setminus A_{1:t-1}(o)}w_{f}, where A t  ( o ) A_{t}(o) is the set of fields disclosed at turn t t and A 1 : t − 1  ( o ) A_{1:t-1}(o) is the set disclosed previously. Thus, retransmitting the same twin state without new fields incurs zero additional disclosure cost. Appendix B Security Proofs This appendix formalizes the security properties stated as design goals in § 3.2. We prove ( k , δ ) (k,\delta) -anonymity under schema projection (Theorem 1), ϵ \epsilon -unlinkability under fresh session IDs (Theorem 2), and a composition bound on cumulative disclosure across T T turns (Theorem 3). B.1. Notation and Setup For the proofs below, we abstract away implementation details and work directly with the projected attribute space induced by the planner-visible abstraction. Let 𝒪 = { o 1 , … , o n } \mathcal{O}={o_{1},\ldots,o_{n}} be the set of real-world objects in the local environment. The schema projection Π \Pi maps each object to a planner-visible representation z = Π  ( o ) z=\Pi(o) drawn from a finite attribute space 𝒵 = 𝒦 × 𝒮 × 2 𝒯 × 2 𝒰 × ℱ × ℛ , \mathcal{Z}=\mathcal{K}\times\mathcal{S}\times 2^{\mathcal{T}}\times 2^{\mathcal{U}}\times\mathcal{F}\times\mathcal{R}, where 𝒦 \mathcal{K} is the set of kind labels ( | 𝒦 | = 6 |\mathcal{K}|=6 ), 𝒮 \mathcal{S} is the set of semantic-class labels, 𝒯 \mathcal{T} is the set of content tags, 𝒰 \mathcal{U} is the set of usability tags, ℱ \mathcal{F} is the set of freshness buckets ( | ℱ | = 3 |\mathcal{F}|=3 ), and ℛ \mathcal{R} is the set of sensitivity levels ( | ℛ | = 3 |\mathcal{R}|=3 ). Let fields  ( z ) \text{fields}(z) denote the set of fields present in z z . For a representation z z with disclosed field subset D ⊆ fields  ( z ) D\subseteq\text{fields}(z) , define the equivalence class (16) EC  ( z , D ) = { o ′ ∈ 𝒪 ∣ Π  ( o ′ ) | D = z | D } , \text{EC}(z,D)={o^{\prime}\in\mathcal{O}\mid\Pi(o^{\prime})|{D}=z|{D}}, that is, the set of objects whose projected representation agrees with z z on all disclosed fields. The disclosure budget for object o o after T T turns is B T  ( o ) = ∑ t = 1 T Δ t  ( o ) B_{T}(o)=\sum_{t=1}^{T}\Delta_{t}(o) , capped by threshold τ  ( o ) \tau(o) (Eq. 11). Auxiliary-knowledge model. For Theorem 1, we model adversarial side information by a random variable ℐ \mathcal{I} . We assume a bounded-likelihood-ratio condition inside each equivalence class: there exists a bound η ≥ 1 \eta\geq 1 such that for every disclosed pattern x x , every o , o ′ ∈ 𝒪 x := EC  ( x , D ) o,o^{\prime}\in\mathcal{O}{x}:=\text{EC}(x,D) , and every realization i i of ℐ \mathcal{I} ,(17)Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ] Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ′ ] ≤ η . \frac{\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o]}{\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o^{\prime}]}\leq\eta.Thus, auxiliary knowledge may bias the posterior within an equivalence class, but cannot arbitrarily single out one member.Cost calibration model.For Theorems 2 and 3, let F t F{t} denote the set of newly disclosed fields about object o o at turn t t , so that Δ t  ( o ) = ∑ f ∈ F t w f \Delta_{t}(o)=\sum_{f\in F_{t}}w_{f} . For each field f f , define its worst-case branching factor (18) b f := max D , x  | { v ∈ 𝒱 f : ∃ o ∈ EC  ( x , D )  with  Π f  ( o ) = v } | , b_{f}:=\max_{D,x}\bigl|{v\in\mathcal{V}{f}:\exists,o\in\text{EC}(x,D)\text{ with }\Pi{f}(o)=v}\bigr|, where 𝒱 f \mathcal{V}{f} is the value domain of field f f , and the maximum ranges over all previously disclosed field sets D D and patterns x x on D D . We assume field costs are calibrated as(19)w f ≥ ln  b f . w{f}\geq\ln b_{f}. Hence, revealing field f f can refine an existing equivalence class by at most a multiplicative factor e w f e^{w_{f}} in the worst case. B.2. Theorem 1: ( k , δ ) (k,\delta) -Anonymity under Schema Projection Theorem 1 (Re-identification Bound). Let the adversary observe z ∗ = Π  ( o ∗ ) z^{}=\Pi(o^{}) with disclosed field set D ∗ D^{} such that ∑ f ∈ D ∗ w f ≤ τ  ( o ∗ ) \sum_{f\in D^{}}w_{f}\leq\tau(o^{}) (enforced by the gatekeeper's per-object budget). Assume:(1) the prior μ \mu is uniform within each equivalence class, that is, conditional on Π  ( O ) | D ∗ = x \Pi(O)|{D^{}}=x , every object in 𝒪 x \mathcal{O}{x} is equally likely a priori; (2) the adversary's side information ℐ \mathcal{I} satisfies the bounded-likelihood-ratio condition ( 17). If | EC  ( z ∗ , D ∗ ) | ≥ k |\text{EC}(z^{},D^{})|\geq k , then for any adversary 𝒜 \mathcal{A} , (20) Pr  [ 𝒜  ( z ∗ | D ∗ , ℐ ) = o ∗ ] ≤ η η + k − 1 . \Pr[\mathcal{A}(z^{}|{D^{}},\mathcal{I})=o^{*}]\leq\frac{\eta}{\eta+k-1}.Equivalently,(21)Pr  [ 𝒜  ( z ∗ | D ∗ , ℐ ) = o ∗ ] ≤ 1 k + δ , \Pr[\mathcal{A}(z^{}|{D^{}},\mathcal{I})=o^{}]\leq\frac{1}{k}+\delta,where(22)δ := η η + k − 1 − 1 k = ( η − 1 )  ( k − 1 ) k  ( η + k − 1 ) . \delta:=\frac{\eta}{\eta+k-1}-\frac{1}{k}=\frac{(\eta-1)(k-1)}{k(\eta+k-1)}.Proof.Fix the disclosed pattern x := z ∗ | D ∗ x:=z^{}|{x}=\text{EC}(z^{},D^{}) , m := | E | m:=|E| . By assumption, m ≥ k m\geq k .Step 1 (Posterior inside the equivalence class). Since all objects in E E induce the same disclosed pattern x x , Bayes' rule gives, for any o ∈ E o\in E and side-information realization i i ,(23)Pr  [ O = o  ∣ Π  ( O ) | D ∗ = x , ℐ = i ] = Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ]  Pr  [ O = o  ∣ Π  ( O ) | D ∗ = x ] ∑ o ′ ∈ E Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ′ ]  Pr  [ O = o ′  ∣ Π  ( O ) | D ∗ = x ] . \begin{split}\Pr[O=o\mid\Pi(O)|{D^{}}=x,\mathcal{I}=i]={}\ \frac{\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o],\Pr[O=o\mid\Pi(O)|{D^{}}=x]}{\sum_{o^{\prime}\in E}\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o^{\prime}],\Pr[O=o^{\prime}\mid\Pi(O)|{D^{}}=x]}.\end{split}By the uniform-within-class prior assumption,Pr  [ O = o  ∣ Π  ( O ) | D ∗ = x ] = 1 m \Pr[O=o\mid\Pi(O)|{D^{}}=x]=\frac{1}{m}for all o ∈ E o\in E . The 1 / m 1/m factors cancel, yielding(24)Pr  [ O = o  ∣ Π  ( O ) | D ∗ = x , ℐ = i ] = Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ] ∑ o ′ ∈ E Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ′ ] . \Pr[O=o\mid\Pi(O)|{D^{}}{=}x,\mathcal{I}{=}i]=\frac{\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o]}{\sum{o^{\prime}\in E}\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o^{\prime}]}. Step 2 (Bounding the maximum posterior mass). Let o ^ ∈ E \hat{o}\in E maximize the posterior in ( 24). By the likelihood-ratio bound ( 17), for every o ′ ∈ E ∖ { o ^ } o^{\prime}\in E\setminus{\hat{o}} , Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ′ ] ≥ 1 η  Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ^ ] . \Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o^{\prime}]\geq\frac{1}{\eta}\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=\hat{o}]. Therefore, (25) ∑ o ′ ∈ E Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ′ ] ≥ Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ^ ] + ( m − 1 ) ⋅ 1 η  Pr  [ ℐ = i ∣ O = o ^ ] . \sum_{o^{\prime}\in E}\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=o^{\prime}]\geq\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=\hat{o}]+(m-1)\cdot\frac{1}{\eta}\Pr[\mathcal{I}=i\mid O=\hat{o}]. Substituting into ( 24), (26) max o ∈ E  Pr  [ O = o ∣ x , i ] ≤ 1 1 + ( m − 1 ) / η = η η + m − 1 . \max_{o\in E}\Pr[O=o\mid x,i]\leq\frac{1}{1+(m-1)/\eta}=\frac{\eta}{\eta+m-1}. Since m ≥ k m\geq k and u ↦ η / ( η + u − 1 ) u\mapsto\eta/(\eta+u-1) is decreasing in u u , (27) max o ∈ E  Pr  [ O = o ∣ x , i ] ≤ η η + k − 1 . \max_{o\in E}\Pr[O=o\mid x,i]\leq\frac{\eta}{\eta+k-1}. Step 3 (Optimal adversary success probability). Given ( x , i ) (x,i) , the optimal re-identification strategy is MAP decoding, whose success probability equals the maximum posterior mass. Hence the bound applies to every adversary 𝒜 \mathcal{A} : Pr  [ 𝒜  ( z ∗ | D ∗ , ℐ ) = o ∗ ] ≤ η η + k − 1 . \Pr[\mathcal{A}(z^{}|{D^{}},\mathcal{I})=o^{*}]\leq\frac{\eta}{\eta+k-1}.The equivalent form with δ \delta follows immediately. ∎Interpretation. When η = 1 \eta=1 , the bound reduces to the classical 1 / k 1/k anonymity guarantee. Empirically, the re-identification experiment in § 5.6 corresponds to the unrestricted case in which all twin fields are disclosed; the budgeted setting limits the disclosed field set and enlarges the resulting equivalence class.B.3. Theorem 2: ϵ \epsilon -Unlinkability across SessionsTheorem 2 (Cross-Session Unlinkability).Let sessions s 1 , s 2 s{1},s_{2} assign fresh random object IDs independently. For a target object o o , let D ( 1 ) D^{(1)} and D ( 2 ) D^{(2)} be the disclosed field sets in the two sessions, and let U := D ( 1 ) ∩ D ( 2 ) U:=D^{(1)}\cap D^{(2)} be the overlap of fields visible in both sessions. Denote the overlap-pattern random variable X U := Π  ( O ) | U X_{U}:=\Pi(O)|{U} , where O O is drawn from prior μ \mu . Assume X U X{U} is uniform on its support 𝒵 U ⊆ ∏ f ∈ U 𝒱 f \mathcal{Z}{U}\subseteq\prod{f\in U}\mathcal{V}{f} , that is,Pr  [ X U = x ] = 1 | 𝒵 U | \Pr[X{U}=x]=\frac{1}{|\mathcal{Z}{U}|}for all x ∈ 𝒵 U x\in\mathcal{Z}{U} . Then for any adversary 𝒜 \mathcal{A} , (28) ln  Pr  [ 𝒜  ( Z ( 1 ) , Z ( 2 ) ) = 1 ∣ same ] Pr  [ 𝒜  ( Z ( 1 ) , Z ( 2 ) ) = 1 ∣ diff ] ≤ ϵ , \ln\frac{\Pr[\mathcal{A}(Z^{(1)},Z^{(2)})=1\mid\text{same}]}{\Pr[\mathcal{A}(Z^{(1)},Z^{(2)})=1\mid\text{diff}]}\leq\epsilon, where ϵ = ln  | 𝒵 U | \epsilon=\ln|\mathcal{Z}{U}| . Moreover, under the cost calibration assumption ( 19) and budget enforcement ∑ f ∈ D ( i ) w f ≤ τ  ( o ) \sum{f\in D^{(i)}}w_{f}\leq\tau(o) , (29) ϵ ≤ ∑ f ∈ U ln  b f ≤ ∑ f ∈ U w f ≤ τ  ( o ) . \epsilon\leq\sum_{f\in U}\ln b_{f}\leq\sum_{f\in U}w_{f}\leq\tau(o). Proof. Let T 1 , T 2 T_{1},T_{2} denote the transcripts seen by the adversary in the two sessions, restricted to the overlap fields U U . Fresh random IDs carry no cross-session information, so any linking rule depends only on the overlap attributes. Step 1 (Distribution under “same” and “diff”). The projection Π \Pi is deterministic, so under the hypothesis same (both sessions involve the same object O O ), both transcripts produce the identical overlap pattern: ( T 1 , T 2 ) = ( X U , X U ) (T_{1},T_{2})=(X_{U},X_{U}) . Under the hypothesis diff (two independent objects O 1 , O 2 ∼ μ O_{1},O_{2}\sim\mu ), the overlap patterns are i.i.d.: ( T 1 , T 2 ) = ( X U ( 1 ) , X U ( 2 ) ) (T_{1},T_{2})=(X_{U}^{(1)},X_{U}^{(2)}) with X U ( 1 ) ⟂ X U ( 2 ) X_{U}^{(1)}\perp X_{U}^{(2)} . Step 2 (Max-divergence computation). For any adversary 𝒜 \mathcal{A} with acceptance region R ⊆ 𝒵 U × 𝒵 U R\subseteq\mathcal{Z}{U}\times\mathcal{Z}{U} , (30) Pr  [ 𝒜 = 1 ∣ same ] = ∑ x : ( x , x ) ∈ R Pr  [ X U = x ] , \Pr[\mathcal{A}=1\mid\text{same}]=\sum_{x:,(x,x)\in R}\Pr[X_{U}=x], (31) Pr [ 𝒜 = 1 ∣ diff ] = ∑ ( x , y ) ∈ R Pr [ X U = x ] Pr [ X U = y ] ≥ ∑ x : ( x , x ) ∈ R Pr [ X U = x ] 2 . \Pr[\mathcal{A}=1\mid\text{diff}]=\sum_{(x,y)\in R}\Pr[X_{U}=x]\Pr[X_{U}=y]\geq\sum_{x:,(x,x)\in R}\Pr[X_{U}=x]^{2}. The inequality holds because the off-diagonal terms in R R only increase the denominator. Under the uniform-on-support assumption, Pr  [ X U = x ] = 1 / N \Pr[X_{U}=x]=1/N where N = | 𝒵 U | N=|\mathcal{Z}{U}| . Hence(32)Pr  [ 𝒜 = 1 ∣ same ] Pr  [ 𝒜 = 1 ∣ diff ] ≤ ∑ x : ( x , x ) ∈ R 1 / N ∑ x : ( x , x ) ∈ R 1 / N 2 = N = | 𝒵 U | . \frac{\Pr[\mathcal{A}=1\mid\text{same}]}{\Pr[\mathcal{A}=1\mid\text{diff}]}\leq\frac{\sum{x:,(x,x)\in R}1/N}{\sum_{x:,(x,x)\in R}1/N^{2}}=N=|\mathcal{Z}{U}|.This bound is tight: the adversary 𝒜 ∗  ( x , y ) = 𝟏  [ x = y = x 0 ] \mathcal{A}^{*}(x,y)=\mathbf{1}[x=y=x{0}] for any fixed x 0 ∈ 𝒵 U x_{0}\in\mathcal{Z}{U} achieves ratio exactly N N .Step 3 (Budget connection). The support size satisfies | 𝒵 U | ≤ ∏ f ∈ U | 𝒱 f | |\mathcal{Z}{U}|\leq\prod{f\in U}|\mathcal{V}{f}| . Under the cost calibration ( 19), | 𝒱 f | ≤ b f ≤ e w f |\mathcal{V}{f}|\leq b{f}\leq e^{w_{f}} , so (33) ϵ = ln  | 𝒵 U | ≤ ∑ f ∈ U ln  | 𝒱 f | ≤ ∑ f ∈ U w f . \epsilon=\ln|\mathcal{Z}{U}|\leq\sum{f\in U}\ln|\mathcal{V}{f}|\leq\sum{f\in U}w_{f}. Since U ⊆ D ( i ) U\subseteq D^{(i)} for each session i i , and the budget enforces ∑ f ∈ D ( i ) w f ≤ τ  ( o ) \sum_{f\in D^{(i)}}w_{f}\leq\tau(o) , we obtain ϵ ≤ τ  ( o ) \epsilon\leq\tau(o) . ∎ Interpretation. The parameter ϵ \epsilon is directly bounded by the overlap of disclosed fields across sessions, and therefore by the disclosure budget τ  ( o ) \tau(o) . Tighter budgets reduce the attribute overlap available for linkage. When no fields overlap across sessions, ϵ = 0 \epsilon=0 and linkage through planner-visible attributes becomes impossible. Remark (non-uniform distributions). For non-uniform X U X_{U} , the worst-case adversary achieves ratio 1 / min x ∈ supp  Pr  [ X U = x ] 1/\min_{x\in\text{supp}}\Pr[X_{U}=x] , giving ϵ = − ln  min x  Pr  [ X U = x ] \epsilon=-\ln\min_{x}\Pr[X_{U}=x] . This can exceed ln  | 𝒵 U | \ln|\mathcal{Z}{U}| when the distribution is skewed. The budget connection still bounds the number of distinguishable overlap patterns, even when their frequencies are not uniform.B.4. Theorem 3: Composition Bound for Multi-Turn DisclosureTheorem 3 (Sequential Composition).Consider an object o o observed across T T turns. Let D t D{t} be the cumulative disclosed field set after turn t t , and let EC t := EC  ( Π  ( o ) , D t ) \text{EC}{t}:=\text{EC}(\Pi(o),D{t}) be the corresponding equivalence class. Assume the field costs satisfy the calibration condition ( 19). Then (34) | EC T | ≥ | EC 0 |  exp  ( − ∑ t = 1 T Δ t  ( o ) ) . |\text{EC}{T}|\geq|\text{EC}{0}|\exp!\Bigl(-\sum_{t=1}^{T}\Delta_{t}(o)\Bigr). In particular, if the gatekeeper enforces B T  ( o ) = ∑ t = 1 T Δ t  ( o ) ≤ τ  ( o ) B_{T}(o)=\sum_{t=1}^{T}\Delta_{t}(o)\leq\tau(o) , then (35) | EC T | ≥ | EC 0 |  e − τ  ( o ) . |\text{EC}{T}|\geq|\text{EC}{0}|,e^{-\tau(o)}. Proof. For each turn t t , let F t := D t ∖ D t − 1 F_{t}:=D_{t}\setminus D_{t-1} be the set of newly disclosed fields, so that Δ t  ( o ) = ∑ f ∈ F t w f \Delta_{t}(o)=\sum_{f\in F_{t}}w_{f} . Step 1 (Monotone shrinkage). Because D t − 1 ⊆ D t D_{t-1}\subseteq D_{t} , every object consistent with the disclosures at turn t t is also consistent at turn t − 1 t-1 . Hence EC t ⊆ EC t − 1 \text{EC}{t}\subseteq\text{EC}{t-1} and | EC t | ≤ | EC t − 1 | |\text{EC}{t}|\leq|\text{EC}{t-1}| . Step 2 (Per-field refinement bound). Fix turn t t and suppose one additional field f ∈ F t f\in F_{t} is revealed. Within the pre-existing class EC t − 1 \text{EC}{t-1} , disclosure of f f partitions that class into at most b f b{f} subclasses by definition of the branching factor ( 18). The subclass selected by the true value of field f f therefore has size at least | EC t − 1 | / b f |\text{EC}{t-1}|/b{f} . Using the cost calibration ( 19), b f ≤ e w f b_{f}\leq e^{w_{f}} , so revealing field f f shrinks the class by at most a factor e w f e^{w_{f}} : (36) | EC ( f ) | ≥ | EC t − 1 |  e − w f . |\text{EC}^{(f)}|\geq|\text{EC}{t-1}|,e^{-w{f}}. Applying this sequentially to all newly revealed fields in F t F_{t} gives (37) | EC t | ≥ | EC t − 1 |  exp  ( − ∑ f ∈ F t w f ) = | EC t − 1 |  e − Δ t  ( o ) . |\text{EC}{t}|\geq|\text{EC}{t-1}|\exp!\Bigl(-\sum_{f\in F_{t}}w_{f}\Bigr)=|\text{EC}{t-1}|,e^{-\Delta{t}(o)}. Step 3 (Sequential composition over T T turns). Iterating ( 37) over t = 1 , … , T t=1,\ldots,T yields (38) | EC T | ≥ | EC 0 |  ∏ t = 1 T e − Δ t  ( o ) = | EC 0 |  exp  ( − ∑ t = 1 T Δ t  ( o ) ) , |\text{EC}{T}|\geq|\text{EC}{0}|\prod_{t=1}^{T}e^{-\Delta_{t}(o)}=|\text{EC}{0}|\exp!\Bigl(-\sum{t=1}^{T}\Delta_{t}(o)\Bigr), which proves ( 34). Budget enforcement B T  ( o ) ≤ τ  ( o ) B_{T}(o)\leq\tau(o) immediately gives ( 35). ∎ Interpretation. Once the budget is exhausted, the gatekeeper either withholds the object from the planner-visible abstraction or refuses further field disclosures. This yields graceful degradation: the planner may lose utility, but the equivalence class is prevented from shrinking beyond the budget-implied bound. Empirical alignment. The multi-turn experiment (§ 5.9) is consistent with this bound: after 58 tasks, NL reaches 1.0 while several objects are excluded by the gatekeeper, demonstrating hard enforcement of the budget cap. PQS degrades only modestly, indicating graceful degradation rather than catastrophic failure. Corollary (product-form bound). Since e − x ≥ 1 − x e^{-x}\geq 1-x for x ≥ 0 x\geq 0 , we also obtain (39) | EC T | ≥ | EC 0 |  ∏ t = 1 T ( 1 − Δ t  ( o ) ) , |\text{EC}{T}|\geq|\text{EC}{0}|\prod_{t=1}^{T}\bigl(1-\Delta_{t}(o)\bigr), whenever each Δ t  ( o ) \Delta_{t}(o) is normalized to lie in [ 0 , 1 ] [0,1] . This product form is weaker but sometimes more intuitive. B.5. Assumptions and Empirical Alignment Table 8 summarizes how each formal result connects to the empirical evaluation and highlights the main modeling assumption behind the corresponding bound. Table 8. Mapping between formal security properties, assumptions, and empirical evidence. The formal guarantees rely on three modeling assumptions: • Bounded side information within equivalence classes (Theorem 1). The likelihood-ratio bound ( 17) limits how strongly auxiliary knowledge can skew the posterior within an equivalence class. When η = 1 \eta=1 , the classical 1 / k 1/k guarantee is recovered exactly. • Uniform overlap-pattern distribution (Theorem 2). Cross-session linkage reduces to distinguishing overlap attribute patterns once fresh IDs remove identifier-based matching. The uniform assumption yields the clean bound ϵ = ln  | 𝒵 U | \epsilon=\ln|\mathcal{Z}{U}| ; skewed distributions can only worsen it.• Field-cost calibration (Theorems 2 and 3). The condition w f ≥ ln  b f w{f}\geq\ln b_{f} ensures that each field cost upper-bounds the logarithmic refinement induced by disclosing that field. This links the unlinkability and composition results to the single budget parameter τ  ( o ) \tau(o) . Experimental support, please view the build logs for errors. Generated by L A T E xml . Go to file Code Open more actions menu Folders and files Repository files navigation README Agentic Guardrails: Multi-Layered Risk-Aware Investment Agent A robust, enterprise-grade autonomous portfolio manager agent capable of reading SEC filings, analyzing market data, and simulating trades. This project focuses on demonstrating multi-layered guardrails (defense-in-depth) to govern AI agents in high-stakes financial environments. 📖 Table of Contents About The Project Key Features Tech Stack Project Structure Core Workflow Getting Started Prerequisites Installation Configuration Usage Roadmap Disclaimer 💡 About The Project This project builds an agent simulating a portfolio manager that can: Read & Analyze: Parse real SEC 10-K filings (e.g., NVIDIA) using EdgarTools . Market Awareness: Query (mocked) real-time market data and rumors. Execute: Propose and simulate trades via a high-risk execute_trade tool. The Goal: The project demonstrates a transition from an unguarded agent (for demonstrating failures) to a heavily governed system. We are progressively implementing Input, Action, and Output Guardrails to mitigate hallucinations, block malicious inputs, and ensure regulatory compliance. Layer 1 (Input Guardrails) and Layer 2 (Plan & Action Guardrails) are now fully active! ✨ Key Features Research Capability: Downloads and extracts relevant context from the latest 10-K filings. Agentic Orchestration: Uses LangGraph for a ReAct-style loop (Reason + Act) to decide on tool usage. Tooling Layer: query_10K_report : Search SEC filings. get_real_time_market_data : Access simulated market feeds. execute_trade : Simulate trade execution. Defense-in-Depth: A structured approach to adding safety layers (Input, Plan, Output) around the LLM. Layer 1 (Input Guardrails) [Active]: Async, parallel execution of Topic Check, Threat Detection (Llama Guard), and Sensitive Data Scanning (PII/MNPI). Layer 2 (Plan & Action Guardrails) [Active]: Plan generation using a designated planner, groundedness checking, dynamically generated policy validation, and Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) manual approval. 🛠 Tech Stack Languages & Runtime Python 3.12 uv (for fast dependency management) Async/await architecture AI / LLM Gemini API ( google-genai ): Core reasoning engine. Ollama: Local model inference (e.g., Gemma 2, Llama Guard) for safety and routing. Frameworks & Libraries LangGraph: Graph-based agent orchestration. LangChain: Message primitives and tool handling. EdgarTools: SEC EDGAR client. Pydantic: Data validation. 📂 Project Structure 🔄 Core Workflow Data Sourcing The data_loader.py script identifies, downloads, and parses the latest 10-K filing for a target ticker. Content is loaded into memory ( TEN_K_REPORT_CONTENT ) for quick access by the agent. Tool Layer ( src/agent/tools.py ) query_10K_report : Keyword search over the filing text. get_real_time_market_data : Returns mocked prices and risk-injected "rumors". execute_trade : Simulates buying/selling stocks. Aegis Layer 1: Input Guardrails Sanitizes the user prompt asynchronously (topic, threat, PII). Planner ( src/agent/planner.py ) Generates a granular action plan based on the sanitized prompt. Aegis Layer 2: Action Guardrails ( src/guardrails/layer2_orchestrator.py ) Validates groundedness. Applies dynamic, AI-generated policy guardrails in real-time. Triggers Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) for high-risk tool calls. Agent Brain ( src/agent/graph.py ) Orchestrator that continues loop execution based only on approved steps. 🚀 Getting Started Prerequisites OS: macOS with Apple Silicon (recommended). Python: Version 3.12+. Tools: uv (package manager), Ollama (for local models). API Keys: Google Gemini API key. Installation Clone the Repository Set up Virtual Environment Install Dependencies Note for macOS users: pygraphviz require C-headers from the graphviz system package to build. Install graphviz first via Homebrew, then link the headers during uv installation: Configuration Create a .env file in the agentic_guardrails/ root directory: Pull Local Models: 💻 Usage
Download Investment Data Initialize the system by downloading the necessary SEC filings: Note: Ensure USER_EMAIL is set in data_loader.py as per SEC requirements.
Run the Guarded Agent Execute the main script to see the agent and its Layer 1 & 2 Guardrails in action: Modify main.py to change the predefined prompt and test different guardrail triggers. Example Layer 2 Scenario (HITL + Policy Validation): "NVDA seems really volatile lately, I'm getting nervous. Maybe do something about my 200 shares?" This prompt passes the Layer 1 checks. The planner deduces a plan to check the market price and execute a sell order. The Layer 2 Guardrails then intercept: Policy Validation Guardrail: Validates the action plan against the generated trading parameters (e.g. avoiding panic selling when dropped above x%). HITL Guardrail: Pauses terminal execution to mandate human override (y/n) before actually proceeding to the execute_trade execution step! 🛣 Roadmap [x] Layer 1: Input Guardrails Filter non-financial topics and PII/MNPI. Prevent malicious instructions using Llama Guard. [x] Layer 2: Plan & Action Guardrails Review agent plans before execution. Enforce "verify before trading" policies. Human-in-the-Loop review. [-] Layer 3: Output Guardrails Hallucination detection (LLM-as-a-judge). Citation verification against source documents. [-] Enhanced Retrieval Implement RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) with a vector database. [-] Web Interface Build a FastAPI backend and a simple UI for interaction. ⚠ Disclaimer This repository is for research and educational purposes only. It is not financial advice. Do not connect this code directly to a real brokerage account without significant additional safety, compliance, and testing layers. About First, a large-scale unguarded AI system is built which is prone to multiple vulnerabilities like security risks, hallucinations, compliance violations, malicious prompts and more. Then the system is transformed into a secured and guarded agentic workflow with addition of security at three different layers.
